itemtype="http://schema.org/BlogPosting" role="article" data-reactid="363"><h1 class="flytitle-and-title__body" data-reactid="364"><span class="flytitle-and-title__flytitle" data-reactid="365">The Senate and the filibuster</span><span class="flytitle-and-title__title" data-reactid="366">Dropping the bomb</span></h1><p class="blog-post__rubric" itemprop="description" data-reactid="367">A change to the Senate’s rulebook will make governing a little easier</p><div class="blog-post__inner" data-reactid="368"><picture class="component-image blog-post__image" data-reactid="369"><source srcset="//web.archive.org/web/20170605014306im_/http://cdn.static-economist.com/sites/default/files/imagecache/180-width/images/print-edition/20131130_USP004_0.jpg 180w, class="component-image__img blog-post__image-block" data-reactid="371"/></picture><div class="blog-post__asideable-wrapper" data-reactid="372"><div class="blog-post__asideable-content blog-post__asideable-content--meta" data-reactid="373"><div class="blog-post__section-date-author" data-reactid="374"><h3 class="blog-post__section" itemprop="articleSection" data-reactid="375"><a itemprop="url" class="blog-post__section-link" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/sections/united-states" data-reactid="376"><span data-reactid="377"><svg role="img" class="Icon Icon-print-edition blog-post__icon-print-edition" width="25px" height="25px" data-reactid="378"><title data-reactid="379">print-edition icon</title><use xlink:href="/web/20170605014306im_/http://www.economist.com/assets/icons--33fb586b88d14cf55598686375498cac05d9f8cc--.svg#print-edition" data-reactid="380"></use></svg><!-- react-text: 381 --> Print edition <!-- /react-text --><!-- react-text: 382 -->| United States<!-- /react-text --></span></a></h3><time class="blog-post__datetime" itemprop="dateCreated" datetime="2013-11-30T00:00:00Z" data-reactid="383">Nov 30th 2013</time><span class="blog-post__location-created" data-reactid="384"> | WASHINGTON, DC </span></div><div class="blog-post__sharebar" data-reactid="385"><div class="share-component" data-reactid="386"><div class="share-component__icons" data-reactid="387"><div class="share__icon share__icon--twitter" data-reactid="388"><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20170605014306/https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?url=" title="Share on Twitter" aria-label="Share on Twitter" class="share__link" data-reactid="389"><svg role="img" share__icon--facebook" data-reactid="393"><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20170605014306/http://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=" title="Share on Facebook" aria-label="Share on Facebook" class="share__link" data-reactid="394"><svg role="img" class="Icon Icon-facebook" width="49px" height="49px" data-reactid="395"><title data-reactid="396">facebook icon</title><use share__icon--linkedin" data-reactid="398"><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20170605014306/https://www.linkedin.com/cws/share?url=" title="LinkedIn" aria-label="LinkedIn" class="share__link" data-reactid="399"><svg role="img" class="Icon Icon-linkedin" width="49px" height="49px" data-reactid="400"><title data-reactid="401">linkedin icon</title><use xlink:href="/web/20170605014306im_/http://www.economist.com/assets/icons--33fb586b88d14cf55598686375498cac05d9f8cc--.svg#linkedin" data-reactid="402"></use></svg></a></div><div class="share__icon share__icon--mail" data-reactid="403"><a href="http://web.archive.org/web/20170605014306/mailto:/?body=" title="Mail" aria-label="Mail" class="share__link" data-reactid="404"><svg role="img" class="Icon Icon-mail" width="49px" height="49px" data-reactid="405"><title data-reactid="406">mail icon</title><use xlink:href="/web/20170605014306im_/http://www.economist.com/assets/icons--33fb586b88d14cf55598686375498cac05d9f8cc--.svg#mail" data-reactid="407"></use></svg></a></div><div class="share__icon share__icon--print" data-reactid="408"><a href="javascript:if(window.print)window.print()" title="Print" aria-label="Print" class="share__link" data-reactid="409"><svg role="img" class="Icon Icon-print" width="49px" height="49px" data-reactid="410"><title data-reactid="411">print icon</title><use xlink:href="/web/20170605014306im_/http://www.economist.com/assets/icons--33fb586b88d14cf55598686375498cac05d9f8cc--.svg#print" data-reactid="412"></use></svg></a></div></div></div></div><a class="blog-post__comments" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/node/21590924/comments" title="Comments" aria-label="Comments" data-reactid="413"><div class="blog-post__comments-icon icon icon--balloon-berlin" data-reactid="414"></div></a></div></div><div class="blog-post__text" itemprop="description" data-reactid="415"><p data-reactid="416">USED in the context of discussions on parliamentary procedure, the “nuclear option” was highly enriched hyperbole. On November 21st it was detonated, as it were. A rule-change passed by the Senate (chief mover Harry Reid, the majority leader) means that the minority party there will no longer be able to filibuster, or talk out, some nominees to the federal judiciary and the executive branch (though the filibuster remains in place for legislation and for nominees to the Supreme Court). Such appointments can now be made with the support of a simple majority. So far there has been no sign of fallout. Nevertheless, the way America governs itself has been altered.</p><p data-reactid="417">Curbing the filibuster, which hands the minority party a powerful tool to thwart the majority, has long been discussed. Both parties refrained from doing it, partly out of a self-interested fear about what would happen when they next found themselves in the minority, partly from some lingering sense that the Senate ought to be a polite place where lawmakers reach across the aisle to make deals. Why did Democrats, who have a majority in the Senate, decide to break with precedent and make the change?</p><div aria-label="Advertisement" class="fe-blogs__blog-post-inline-ad fe-blogs__mobile-ad ad-panel__container ad-panel__container--styled ad-panel__container--block" role="complementary" itemscope="" data-reactid="418"><div class="ad-panel__googlead" title="Advertisement" id="ad-panel0.04683022376159074" data-reactid="419"></div></div><div class="latest-updates-panel__container latest-updates-panel__container--blog-post" data-reactid="420"><div months</h3></div></a><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__footer" data-reactid="429"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card__subtitle" data-reactid="445"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card__subtitle" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/blogs/democracyinamerica" data-reactid="446">Democracy in America</a><time class="latest-updates-panel-card__time" data-reactid="447">2 days ago</time></div></article></li><li class="latest-updates-panel-card__wrapper" data-reactid="448"><article class="latest-updates-panel__article-link" data-reactid="449"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/news/europe/21722989-leo-varadkar-known-speaking-his-mind-not-always-wisely-ireland-looks-set-young-gay" data-reactid="450"><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__body" data-reactid="451"><h3 class="latest-updates-panel-card__title" data-reactid="452">Ireland looks set for a young, gay prime minister</h3></div></a><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__footer" data-reactid="453"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card__subtitle" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/sections/europe" data-reactid="454">Europe</a><time class="latest-updates-panel-card__time" data-reactid="455">2 days ago</time></div></article></li><li class="latest-updates-panel-card__wrapper" data-reactid="456"><article class="latest-updates-panel__article-link" data-reactid="457"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/blogs/graphicdetail/2017/06/polls-polls-polls" data-reactid="458"><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__body" data-reactid="459"><h3 class="latest-updates-panel-card__title" data-reactid="460">Are British pollsters headed towards another miss?</h3></div></a><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__footer" data-reactid="461"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card__subtitle" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/blogs/graphicdetail" data-reactid="462">Graphic detail</a><time class="latest-updates-panel-card__time" data-reactid="463">2 days ago</time></div></article></li><li class="latest-updates-panel-card__wrapper" data-reactid="464"><article class="latest-updates-panel__article-link" data-reactid="465"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/blogs/democracyinamerica/2017/06/final-appeal" data-reactid="466"><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__body" data-reactid="467"><h3 class="latest-updates-panel-card__title" data-reactid="468">Donald Trump asks the Supreme Court to reinstate his travel ban </h3></div></a><div class="latest-updates-panel-card__footer" data-reactid="469"><a itemprop="url" class="latest-updates-panel-card__subtitle" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/blogs/democracyinamerica" data-reactid="470">Democracy in America</a><time class="latest-updates-panel-card__time" data-reactid="471">2 days ago</time></div></article></li></ul><div class="latest-updates-panel__footer latest-updates-panel__footer--blog-post" data-reactid="472"><a class="link-button link-button-standalone-read-more link-button-small" href="/web/20170605014306/http://www.economist.com/latest-updates" data-reactid="473">See all updates</a></div></div><p data-reactid="474">The move reflects general gloom at the prospect of getting anything significant done before the next electoral cycle. Because there are no big bills likely to pass Congress, there is little incentive to keep things civil. Senate Democrats would rather get some people they approve of into important positions.</p><p data-reactid="475">There is also an important payoff for making the change now. There are close to 100 vacancies in the federal judiciary to be filled. That number is partly coincidence: presidents have limited control over when judges die, retire or get promoted. But it also reflects the fact that Republicans have been more active in blocking the president’s judicial nominees than Democrats were when Republicans last held the White House. The success rate for nominees under George W. Bush was 91%. Under Barack Obama it has fallen to 76%—and those numbers do not reflect the amount of time nominees have had to wait before confirmation.</p><p data-reactid="476">Something similar has been going on in the executive branch. Preventing presidential nominees from taking up their positions in government agencies has become a favourite way for Republican senators to put leg-irons on the president’s policies. This tactic has been successful in preventing the Federal Labour Relations Authority from doing anything; it has also temporarily denied agencies, from the Federal Housing Finance Agency to the CIA, of confirmed heads, without whom they may drift along in an ineffectual way that pleases some small-government types. Such blocking often bears no relation to whether the nominee is qualified. Chuck Hagel, a Vietnam veteran, recipient of two Purple Hearts and former Republican senator, was blocked by his former colleagues from heading the Department of Defence.</p><p data-reactid="477">Though this is not new, it has become much more common under Mr Obama. Between 1949 and Mr Obama taking office, 68 nominees had been blocked by the Senate. Since January 2009, 79 have been. The blockers justify this by pointing to Mr Obama’s habit of using government agencies to make policy, rather than merely carry it out. Mr Obama’s response has been to make some appointments while the Senate is on holiday, which looks equally unconstitutional.</p><p data-reactid="478"><!-- react-text: 479 -->To the extent that getting rid of the filibuster for some nominations reflects a further decline in the civility that was once thought to characterise the Senate, the change is regrettable. But <!-- /react-text --><em class="Italic" data-reactid="480">politesse </em><!-- react-text: 481 -->was dropped some time ago. It makes sense to update the rules accordingly. If that means Republicans find it a little easier to get their way when they eventually win a majority, that is no bad thing.<!-- /react-text --></p></div><div data-reactid="482"><div id="piano__in-line-paywall" data-reactid="483"></div></div></div><div class="blog-post__bottom-panel" data-reactid="484"><div class="blog-post__bottom-panel-top" data-reactid="485"><div 30th 2013, 00:00","children":{"entries":[]},"rubric":"A change to the Senate’s rulebook will make governing a little easier","flyTitle":"The Senate and the filibuster","html":[{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"0"},"children":["USED in the context of discussions on parliamentary procedure, the “nuclear option” was highly enriched hyperbole. On November 21st it was detonated, as it were. A rule-change passed by the Senate (chief mover Harry Reid, the majority leader) means that the minority party there will no longer be able to filibuster, or talk out, some nominees to the federal judiciary and the executive branch (though the filibuster remains in place for legislation and for nominees to the Supreme Court). Such appointments can now be made with the support of a simple majority. So far there has been no sign of fallout. Nevertheless, the way America governs itself has been altered."]},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"1"},"children":["Curbing the filibuster, which hands the minority party a powerful tool to thwart the majority, has long been discussed. Both parties refrained from doing it, partly out of a self-interested fear about what would happen when they next found themselves in the minority, partly from some lingering sense that the Senate ought to be a polite place where lawmakers reach across the aisle to make deals. Why did Democrats, who have a majority in the Senate, decide to break with precedent and make the change?"]},{"type":"inline_ad","key":"2"},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"3"},"children":["The move reflects general gloom at the prospect of getting anything significant done before the next electoral cycle. Because there are no big bills likely to pass Congress, there is little incentive to keep things civil. Senate Democrats would rather get some people they approve of into important positions."]},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"4"},"children":["There is also an important payoff for making the change now. There are close to 100 vacancies in the federal judiciary to be filled. That number is partly coincidence: presidents have limited control over when judges die, retire or get promoted. But it also reflects the fact that Republicans have been more active in blocking the president’s judicial nominees than Democrats were when Republicans last held the White House. The success rate for nominees under George W. Bush was 91%. Under Barack Obama it has fallen to 76%—and those numbers do not reflect the amount of time nominees have had to wait before confirmation."]},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"5"},"children":["Something similar has been going on in the executive branch. Preventing presidential nominees from taking up their positions in government agencies has become a favourite way for Republican senators to put leg-irons on the president’s policies. This tactic has been successful in preventing the Federal Labour Relations Authority from doing anything; it has also temporarily denied agencies, from the Federal Housing Finance Agency to the CIA, of confirmed heads, without whom they may drift along in an ineffectual way that pleases some small-government types. Such blocking often bears no relation to whether the nominee is qualified. Chuck Hagel, a Vietnam veteran, recipient of two Purple Hearts and former Republican senator, was blocked by his former colleagues from heading the Department of Defence."]},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"6"},"children":["Though this is not new, it has become much more common under Mr Obama. Between 1949 and Mr Obama taking office, 68 nominees had been blocked by the Senate. Since January 2009, 79 have been. The blockers justify this by pointing to Mr Obama’s habit of using government agencies to make policy, rather than merely carry it out. Mr Obama’s response has been to make some appointments while the Senate is on holiday, which looks equally unconstitutional."]},{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"p","attributes":{"key":"7"},"children":["To the extent that getting rid of the filibuster for some nominations reflects a further decline in the civility that was once thought to characterise the Senate, the change is regrettable. But ",{"type":"html-tag","tagname":"em","attributes":{"className":"Italic","key":"1"},"children":["politesse "]},"was dropped some time ago. It makes sense to update the rules accordingly.
